href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cells" title="Stem cells">stem cell</a> precursors, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_migration" title="Cellular migration">migration</a> of immature neurons from their birthplaces in the embryo to their final positions, outgrowth of <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a> from neurons and <a href="/wiki/Axon_guidance" title="Axon guidance">guidance</a> of the motile <a href="/wiki/Growth_cone" title="Growth cone">growth cone</a> through the embryo towards postsynaptic partners, the generation of <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a> between these axons and their postsynaptic partners, and finally the lifelong <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">changes</a> in synapses which are thought to underlie learning and memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh52_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh52-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>All bilaterian animals at an early stage of development form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrula" title="Gastrula">gastrula</a>, which is polarized, with one end called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_pole" title="Animal pole">animal pole</a> and the other the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetal_pole" title="Vegetal pole">vegetal pole</a>.  The gastrula has the shape of a disk with three layers of cells, an inner layer called the <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a>, which gives rise to the lining of most internal organs, a middle layer called the <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, which gives rise to the bones and muscles, and an outer layer called the <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a>, which gives rise to the skin and nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SanesCh1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-SanesCh1-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table align="center">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray17.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Gray17.png/250px-Gray17.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Gray17.png/375px-Gray17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Gray17.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray17.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human embryo, showing neural groove</div></div></div></div>\n</td>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:427px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Development_of_the_neural_tube.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="598" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Development_of_the_neural_tube.png/425px-Development_of_the_neural_tube.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Development_of_the_neural_tube.png 1.5x" width="425"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Development_of_the_neural_tube.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four stages in the development of the neural tube in the human embryo</div></div></div></div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In vertebrates, the first sign of the nervous system is the appearance of a thin strip of cells along the center of the back, called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_plate" title="Neural plate">neural plate</a>.  The inner portion of the neural plate (along the midline) is destined to become the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS), the outer portion the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> (PNS).  As development proceeds, a fold called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_groove" title="Neural groove">neural groove</a> appears along the midline.  This fold deepens, and then closes up at the top.  At this point the future CNS appears as a cylindrical structure called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_tube" title="Neural tube">neural tube</a>, whereas the future PNS appears as two strips of tissue called the <a href="/wiki/Neural_crest" title="Neural crest">neural crest</a>, running lengthwise above the neural tube.  The sequence of stages from neural plate to neural tube and neural crest is known as <a href="/wiki/Neurulation" title="Neurulation">neurulation</a>.\n</p><p>In the early 20th century, a set of famous experiments by Hans Spemann and Hilde Mangold showed that the formation of nervous tissue is "induced" by signals from a group of mesodermal cells called the <i>organizer region</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh52_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh52-72">[72]</a></sup> For decades, though, the nature of the induction process defeated every attempt to figure it out, until finally it was resolved by genetic approaches in the 1990s.  Induction of neural tissue requires inhibition of the gene for a so-called <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein" title="Bone morphogenetic protein">bone morphogenetic protein</a>, or BMP.  Specifically the protein <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BMP4" title="BMP4">BMP4</a> appears to be involved.  Two proteins called <a href="/wiki/Noggin_(protein)" title="Noggin (protein)">Noggin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chordin" title="Chordin">Chordin</a>, both secreted by the mesoderm, are capable of inhibiting BMP4 and thereby inducing ectoderm to turn into neural tissue.  It appears that a similar molecular mechanism is involved for widely disparate types of animals, including arthropods as well as vertebrates.  In some animals, however, another type of molecule called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibroblast_Growth_Factor" title="Fibroblast Growth Factor">Fibroblast Growth Factor</a> or FGF may also play an important role in induction.\n</p><p>Induction of neural tissues causes formation of neural precursor cells, called <a href="/wiki/Neuroblast" title="Neuroblast">neuroblasts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KandelCh53_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-KandelCh53-74">[74]</a></sup>  In drosophila, neuroblasts divide asymmetrically, so that one product is a "ganglion mother cell" (GMC), and the other is a neuroblast.  A GMC divides once, to give rise to either a pair of neurons or a pair of glial cells.  In all, a neuroblast is capable of generating an indefinite number of neurons or glia.\n</p><p>As shown in a 2008 study, one factor common to all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_(biology)" title="Symmetry (biology)">bilateral</a> organisms (including humans) is a family of secreted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signalling_molecule" title="Signalling molecule">signaling molecules</a> called <a href="/wiki/Neurotrophin" title="Neurotrophin">neurotrophins</a> which regulate the growth and survival of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhu_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu-75">[75]</a></sup>  Zhu et al. identified DNT1, the first neurotrophin found in <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a>. DNT1 shares structural similarity with all known neurotrophins and is a key factor in the fate of neurons in <a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a>. Because neurotrophins have now been identified in both vertebrate and invertebrates, this evidence suggests that neurotrophins were present in an ancestor common to bilateral organisms and may represent a common mechanism for nervous system formation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathology">Pathology</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">Neurology</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">Psychiatry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNS_protection.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CNS_protection.jpg/267px-CNS_protection.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CNS_protection.jpg/401px-CNS_protection.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CNS_protection.jpg/534px-CNS_protection.jpg 2x" width="267"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNS_protection.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Layers protecting the brain and spinal cord.</div></div></div>\n<p>The central nervous system is protected by major physical and chemical barriers.  Physically, the brain and spinal cord are surrounded by tough <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meningeal</a> membranes, and enclosed in the bones of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, which combine to form a strong physical shield.  Chemically, the brain and spinal cord are isolated by the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood&#8211;brain barrier">blood&#8211;brain barrier</a>, which prevents most types of chemicals from moving from the bloodstream into the interior of the CNS.  These protections make the CNS less susceptible in many ways than the PNS; the flip side, however, is that damage to the CNS tends to have more serious consequences.\n</p><p>Although nerves tend to lie deep under the skin except in a few places such as the <a href="/wiki/Ulnar_nerve" title="Ulnar nerve">ulnar nerve</a> near the elbow joint, they are still relatively exposed to physical damage, which can cause pain, loss of sensation, or loss of muscle control.  Damage to nerves can also be caused by swelling or bruises at places where a nerve passes through a tight bony channel, as happens in <a href="/wiki/Carpal_tunnel_syndrome" title="Carpal tunnel syndrome">carpal tunnel syndrome</a>.  If a nerve is completely transected, it will often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_regeneration" title="Nerve regeneration">regenerate</a>, but for long nerves this process may take months to complete.  In addition to physical damage, <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_neuropathy" title="Peripheral neuropathy">peripheral neuropathy</a> may be caused by many other medical problems, including genetic conditions, metabolic conditions such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, inflammatory conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain&#8211;Barr&#233; syndrome">Guillain&#8211;Barr&#233; syndrome</a>, vitamin deficiency, infectious diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shingles" title="Shingles">shingles</a>, or poisoning by toxins such as heavy metals.  Many cases have no cause that can be identified, and are referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idiopathic" title="Idiopathic">idiopathic</a>.  It is also possible for nerves to lose function temporarily, resulting in numbness as stiffness&#8212;common causes include mechanical pressure, a drop in temperature, or chemical interactions with <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthetic" title="Local anesthetic">local anesthetic</a> drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Lidocaine" title="Lidocaine">lidocaine</a>.\n</p><p>Physical damage to the spinal cord may result in <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">loss of sensation or movement</a>.  If an injury to the spine produces nothing worse than swelling, the symptoms may be transient, but if nerve fibers in the spine are actually destroyed, the loss of function is usually permanent.  Experimental studies have shown that spinal nerve fibers attempt to regrow in the same way as nerve fibers, but in the spinal cord, tissue destruction usually produces scar tissue that cannot be penetrated by the regrowing nerves.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Tortora, G.J., Derrickson, B. (2016). <i>Principles of Anatomy and Physiology (15th edition)</i>. J. Wiley. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-119-34373-8"
